    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9855656922712503209">This unforgettable memoir, by one of our most gifted writers, introduces us to the young Toby Wolff, by turns tough and vulnerable, crafty and bumbling, and ultimately winning. Separated by divorce from his father and brother, Toby and his mother are constantly on the move, yet they develop an extraordinarily close, almost telepathic relationship. As Toby fights for identi</span>
  <span id="freeText9855656922712503209" style="display:none">This unforgettable memoir, by one of our most gifted writers, introduces us to the young Toby Wolff, by turns tough and vulnerable, crafty and bumbling, and ultimately winning. Separated by divorce from his father and brother, Toby and his mother are constantly on the move, yet they develop an extraordinarily close, almost telepathic relationship. As Toby fights for identity and self-respect against the unrelenting hostility of a new stepfather, his experiences are at once poignant and comical, and Wolff does a masterful job of re-creating the frustrations and cruelties of adolescence. His various schemes - running away to Alaska, forging checks, and stealing cars - lead eventually to an act of outrageous self-invention that releases him into a new world of possibility.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9855656922712503209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestionSubTitle'>
Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1304829-tobbias-writes-one-morning-i-woke-up-to">Tobbias writes: &quot;One morning I woke up to this man embracing me and making declarations of love. I got him out of the apartment and called my father who told me to &quot;shoot the bastard&#39;&quot; (283). This part startled me. Toby is in his teens alone and man starts practically stalking him. Why did Toby put this in the book? Does it have any meaning that I&#39;m missing? Maybe describing the bad conditions of his father&#39;s home.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1304829'><a id="like_count_community_question_1304829" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1304829?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F11466.This_Boy_s_Life">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1304829-tobbias-writes-one-morning-i-woke-up-to">2 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1304829-tobbias-writes-one-morning-i-woke-up-to">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/84711462-mick-guinn"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1535915949i/84711462._UX100_CR0,12,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/84711462-mick-guinn">Mick Guinn</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'm just going to speculate here, because it's an interesting question, but while Tobias is 15 years older than me, we had some similar experiences in<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'm just going to speculate here, because it's an interesting question, but while Tobias is 15 years older than me, we had some similar experiences in the search for male mentorship. There is even the sense that a bad male mentor (Dwight) is better than none, as Wolff's mother seems to indicate. <br /><br />For me, Wolff’s brief reference to this event is telling for a couple of reasons (at least for me). <br /><br />Homosexuality was thought to be one of the scariest aspects of boyhood in growing up. I remember clearly worrying, once we understood what it was in our early teens, that we'd turn gay. There was little enlightened thought on this topic in the 70s among boys. On top of that, it's statistically very common for younger boys as they're coming of age to begin to play with each other in sexual ways. Jack has this experience, brief though it may be, when he kisses Arthur presumably out of curiosity. <br /><br />"One night he kissed me, or I kissed him, or we kissed each other. It surprised us both. After that, whenever we felt particularly close, we turned on each other."<br /><br />If Wolff’s experience was in any way similar to my own, he probably felt shame and fear about these early innocent and natural explorations that happened before it was clear this was a very “wrong” or “unmanly” thing for boys to do. The sins of the 12 year old come back to haunt the 15-year old.<br /><br />Arthur being called a “sissy” out loud is also a “fighting word.” For me, he validates the culture at the time that this was about as bad a thing as you could call another boy. If you didn’t fight about it, you were basically acknowledging it must be true. Wolff never says whether Arthur grows up and eventually comes out as a gay man, but I think it’s implied, especially as he writes about how Arthur treats his “girlfriend.” <br /><br />As to my own experience of pulling away from my father as he fell down repeatedly from his job of parenting after my mother left him, I found myself in more risky situations than I might have been. I was out more at night and just starting to enjoy beer and pot. I was out at night with or without his knowledge and I found myself with older teenagers and some men. I recall quite clearly being the target of men who would “come on” to me. Whether it was flirtatious talk laden with innuendo, or an actual hand on the thigh, it was pretty scary when I stupidly realized what was happening. I’d wager that my experience is not wholly uncommon for a lot of boys becoming men.<br /><br />When the incident in question occurs, Wolff is older, but still a boy who has yet again been abandoned by the older, presumedly wiser men. Geoffrey, his 6 year older brother is gone, and his birthfather has abandoned him. Blood abandons. When this friend they leave him with makes a move on Tobias, it’s just another betrayal from a man in power. It’s a different kind of betrayal, but just as powerful in its own way.<br /><br />I think Wolff includes this incident to both indicate another abandonment by family (everyone in his family abandoned him, including his mother to the sadist Dwight), another betrayal of trust from a guardian (in this case sexual assault rather than being beaten up), and cadence of sorts to his relationship with the guy who should have been his best friend for life, Arthur Gayle. One wonders if “Gayle” is really Arthur’s last name, or Wolff used it to confirm our suspicions about his sexual preference. <br /><br />Great question. Not sure if my musings are correct, but it was good think more deeply about this moment that did sort of fly by. <br /><br />~ <br /><br /><a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2610536660" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2610536660" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2610536660?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=biography-biographical">biography-biographical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2610536660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16515881126232370590"><b>How Do Any of Us Survive?</b><br/><br/>This is the fourth memoir of a less than ideal childhood I’ve read in as many weeks. I have to say it’s getting a bit old, all this overcoming adversity stuff. But I think it’s safe to say that Tolstoy had it wrong: Unhappy families are just as unvarying and just as routine as happy ones. But at least occasionally the unhappy ones are interesting, sometimes even revelatory. <br/><br/>Wolfe’s memoir is interesting to me because he understands how his childhood shaped his culture - </span>
  <span id="freeText16515881126232370590" style="display:none"><b>How Do Any of Us Survive?</b><br /><br />This is the fourth memoir of a less than ideal childhood I’ve read in as many weeks. I have to say it’s getting a bit old, all this overcoming adversity stuff. But I think it’s safe to say that Tolstoy had it wrong: Unhappy families are just as unvarying and just as routine as happy ones. But at least occasionally the unhappy ones are interesting, sometimes even revelatory. <br /><br />Wolfe’s memoir is interesting to me because he understands how his childhood shaped his culture - not just his specific fears and aspirations (mostly about violence) but also his responses to the world in general. His childhood, as that of all of us, created the character we attribute to the universe as well as our own. To ‘get’ that and consider both aspects of character may be about as close to maturity as we can get.<br /><br />So, for example, Wolff is able to articulate a basic relation which the child has intuited: <i>“Power can be enjoyed only when it is recognized and feared. Fearlessness in those without power is maddening to those who have it.” </i>The implication of course is a sort of Don Quixote self-image which incites and justifies all sorts of boyish bad behavior. But it is also worth noting that he is talking about a relationship here, and therefore a dynamic explanation, a theory of the world and how it works. This is what’s called a zero-sun relation: if you win, I lose. Therefore, if I can’t win, I make sure you can’t either. Pretty sophisticated stuff. But then children are always more sophisticated than adults remember.<br /><br />To appreciate that one’s life has been shaped by a rather clever inference about the world is clearly a sort of breakthrough. The fact that others (like me) might have adopted similar theories of living is probably sufficient justification for publication. The theory allowed him to negotiate life as a boy and as a young man; it helped Wolff to survive. In short, Wolff’s puerile logic worked - power could be withstood, and even occasionally overcome. Not bad for a boy from a broken family, growing up poor and with no obvious prospects.<br /><br />But of course the very success of this strategy for contending with power masks a deeper issue which is also very practical but by its nature must be raised in philosophical language: Is one’s life best spent contending with power? Success in beating power at its own game may be considered successful merely because of the deprivations one has experienced. Couldn’t it be that it is just this criterion of success which has to be overcome, that perhaps the world isn’t primarily an arena of power exercised and power subverted or deflected?<br /><br />This is not an easy thing to even allow into consciousness much less address. The last thing any of us wants to do is question what we have implicitly lived our lives for. It takes a particular sort of lonely courage to permit it to percolate up through layers of experience acquired and confirmed over decades. What we value is its own measure of success after all. If power is what we have valued, giving it up as unimportant feels like giving up one’s life.<br /><br />Because the memoir ends in late adolescence, there is only a hint that Wolff’s worldview of power and its control is starting to crack. He has a horrifying thought at one point, for example, that his behavior just might be a <i>“solemn choreography of earnest useless acts.” </i>And that perhaps the spectrum of power-relationships he has inferred doesn’t exhaust the range of human life. <i>“It takes a childish or corrupt imagination to make symbols of other people,”</i> he says. He knows he must somehow redeem himself, but that in order to have a <i>“hope of [another’s idea of] redemption I would have to give up my own.”</i><br /><br />A bird in the hand means one can’t do anything else with that hand. So it’s likely that most of us go through life self-handicapped by our successes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16515881126232370590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_736168667" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/736168667" />
    <a title="Jeff " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12618229-jeff"><img alt="Jeff " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529089518p2/12618229.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/736168667?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff " href="/user/show/12618229-jeff">Jeff </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12618229-jeff?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer736168667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11141649295911943820">Part of moving from being a teenager to a functional adult is seeking your own identity outside of what friends and family think of you. Tobias Wolff’s struggle with this is in part what makes this book such a great read. Although he grew up in 1950’s Washington state and his life experiences are somewhat different from mine, it’s the core of feelings of being a teenager that never change and are the same no matter what your circumstances.<br/><br/>Part of what makes Wolff’s struggle that much harder is t</span>
  <span id="freeText11141649295911943820" style="display:none">Part of moving from being a teenager to a functional adult is seeking your own identity outside of what friends and family think of you. Tobias Wolff’s struggle with this is in part what makes this book such a great read. Although he grew up in 1950’s Washington state and his life experiences are somewhat different from mine, it’s the core of feelings of being a teenager that never change and are the same no matter what your circumstances.<br /><br />Part of what makes Wolff’s struggle that much harder is that his mother makes poor choices of the men she brings into their lives. When the book opens, she’s on the road trying to run from a bad relationship, with Tobias in tow. A truck passes them; it has brake troubles. The truck crashes and burns. This is an apt metaphor for Wolff’s life as it’s related in the book. It’s listed on the cover as an autobiography, but it avoids the recitation of facts and reads like a novel.<br /><br />What made Wolff’s adolescence harder was a cruel and demanding step-father. It’s been a long time since I wanted to throttle a character from a book, but Dwight, his step-father, merits these strong feelings. As someone who grew up with an abusive father, I can relate to Wolff’s struggle to maintain his “self” as an overbearing parent heartlessly heaps abuse upon abuse.<br /><br />Wolff tells that when he speaks to his children in anger, he can recognize Dwight’s voice as his own. His youngest daughter, shocked by the tone of his voice, asked him if he loved her.<br /><br />The book isn’t just a grim litany of bad times; Wolff relates his problems with a fair amount of wit and humor. The problems Wolff faced aren’t neatly resolved in this book either.<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11141649295911943820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2104630468" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2104630468" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2104630468?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=autobiography">autobiography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2104630468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1842749279772905372">I don't know if I've been specifically targeting good reads subconsciously or if I've just been lucky that they're falling into my lap. Regardless, the kinda funny, a little sad, quite insightful <i>This Boy's Life</i> by Tobias Wolff struck the old chord with me and continued that trend. Long may it last!<br/><br/>As a somewhat rudderless boy myself I enjoyed this story of a somewhat rudderless boy growing up with only a transient mother and the occasional uncaring, abusive stepfather. This is a fairly typical </span>
  <span id="freeText1842749279772905372" style="display:none">I don't know if I've been specifically targeting good reads subconsciously or if I've just been lucky that they're falling into my lap. Regardless, the kinda funny, a little sad, quite insightful <i>This Boy's Life</i> by Tobias Wolff struck the old chord with me and continued that trend. Long may it last!<br /><br />As a somewhat rudderless boy myself I enjoyed this story of a somewhat rudderless boy growing up with only a transient mother and the occasional uncaring, abusive stepfather. This is a fairly typical coming-of-age tale, which in this case includes vignettes on getting into fights, making and breaking friendships, girls and their potential for a horny young man, trying to be cool, cars, guns, etc and then some. <br /><br />Published in '89, this feels a whole lot older. Probably because it mostly describes things that happened in the late '50s and early 60s. It reminds me a bit of <i>A Christmas Story</i> in that way, just more morbid. Perhaps likening it the tv show "The Wonder Years" would be more to the mark. Yes, just think of the young Tobias as a more real, less Hollywood-chipper Kevin Arnold.<br /><br />Wolff's prose is a joy to read. Every once in a while he lays down a sweet-ass line that makes ya go "hmmm". *<i>does the Arsenio move</i>* There were times when I got quite lost in his words. However, this is a particularly intimate memoir and there are a few intense moments that draw you right into the scene, making you hold your breath and possibly pray for a positive outcome. That's quality writing.<br /><br />While I doubt this will be a five star book for everyone, Wolfe's writing style and the stories he told were utterly relatable in my mind. The book felt familiar to me and some of the aspects of my own coming-of-age story. However, even readers who can't relate personally to the content should still be able to derive a good deal of enjoyment from it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1842749279772905372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2159667340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9567648838317401776">Life is a turkey shoot<br/><br/>Yeah, you know, sometimes you shoot so well and win the turkey, sometimes you lose, and sometimes there isn't even any turkey. You could try to be honest and keep your nose clean, but it doesn't guarantee anything. A screwed-up kid who lies, cheats, forges documents, drinks, and steals, not to mention damaging property, gets kicked out of schools, but winds up with a degree from Oxford, a nationally-famous writer and professor at one of the best universities. He writes clea</span>
  <span id="freeText9567648838317401776" style="display:none">Life is a turkey shoot<br /><br />Yeah, you know, sometimes you shoot so well and win the turkey, sometimes you lose, and sometimes there isn't even any turkey. You could try to be honest and keep your nose clean, but it doesn't guarantee anything. A screwed-up kid who lies, cheats, forges documents, drinks, and steals, not to mention damaging property, gets kicked out of schools, but winds up with a degree from Oxford, a nationally-famous writer and professor at one of the best universities. He writes clear, strong prose that stays with you for a long time. Read this book or "In Pharaoh's Army" or some of his short stories. This is the story of his teenage years, mostly in Washington state, in a town called Concrete. An absent father, a Pollyannaish mother, and an abusive stepfather all created a kind of hell on earth for Tobias Wolff. But when you're that age, you're not sure. Maybe that's the way it's supposed to be. This is a book that doesn't excuse himself, it's just all there in black and white. You could see the (1993) movie too, with Leonardo di Caprio in the title role, and with Robert de Niro as the abusive stepfather. Ellen Barkin is lovely as the mother. But the book is far richer. Tobias never got to even compete in that turkey shoot way back then, but I reckon he got the prize in Life. It's definitely an American classic along with his book on Vietnam.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9567648838317401776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer816807370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15548214930669710260">Find all of my reviews at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/'>http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br/><br/>I read this book almost two months ago and have struggled to come up with some kind (any kind) of review. Sometimes when I read a memoir I’m struck with the question “what made this person think their personal history was novel worthy?” Such is the case with <i>This Boy’s Life</i>. Sure Tobias Wolf had a shitty childhood, but when compared to other autobiographies (<i>Night</i> stands out as the most monumental personal history I can think of, or ev</span>
  <span id="freeText15548214930669710260" style="display:none">Find all of my reviews at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/">http://52bookminimum.blogspot.com/</a><br><br>I read this book almost two months ago and have struggled to come up with some kind (any kind) of review. Sometimes when I read a memoir I’m struck with the question “what made this person think their personal history was novel worthy?” Such is the case with <i>This Boy’s Life</i>. Sure Tobias Wolf had a shitty childhood, but when compared to other autobiographies (<i>Night</i> stands out as the most monumental personal history I can think of, or even <i>The Glass Castle</i> as the most bizarre. Those are books that must have begged to be written). Whatever the reasoning behind Wolff’s decision to write his story, I’m glad he did. <br><br>I believe Wolff’s honesty while telling his tale is both what made this book so memorable and made it so hard for me to summarize. Not only does he tell us about his stepfather, who was a real piece of work, but he is also honest about the fact that he was more than a handful himself during his formative years. While I wanted to jump into the pages and beat the living hell out of Dwight, at times I also wanted to jump in to wash Wolff’s mouth out with soap or give him a good throttling.<br><br>This book is so well-written and reads like fiction. A truly unforgettable coming-of-age story. If you’re generally not the sort who reads non-fiction, this is a great choice. <br><br><br>Sidenote: The movie adaptation features a fresh faced young boy who will give us a glimpse of the brilliant career that is to follow . . . <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/cy2c8FM" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1396374975ra/9124676.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15548214930669710260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202938621?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=aere-perennius">aere-perennius</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202938621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2642073423080083294"><i>“Knowing that everything comes to an end is a gift of experience, a consolation gift for knowing that we ourselves are coming to an end."</i><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1467526106i/19601527._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='267' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/><i>"Before we get it we live in a continuous present, and imagine the future as more of that present. Happiness is endless happiness, innocent of its own sure passing. Pain is endless pain.”</i> <br/>― Tobias Wolff, This Boy's Life<br/><br/>One of my favorite memoirs of all time. IT was perfect in its pacing, its pitch. It was a beautiful, but unsentimental look at youth, poverty, </span>
  <span id="freeText2642073423080083294" style="display:none"><i>“Knowing that everything comes to an end is a gift of experience, a consolation gift for knowing that we ourselves are coming to an end."</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1467526106i/19601527._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="267" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><i>"Before we get it we live in a continuous present, and imagine the future as more of that present. Happiness is endless happiness, innocent of its own sure passing. Pain is endless pain.”</i> <br>― Tobias Wolff, This Boy's Life<br><br>One of my favorite memoirs of all time. IT was perfect in its pacing, its pitch. It was a beautiful, but unsentimental look at youth, poverty, family, and all the cracks and fissures that the world creates to swallow the dreams of youth. Wolff's language still rings with me. I find myself, going back and reading whole passages of 'This Boy's Life' just to drink the language and the rub against the energy and charge of Wolff's vitality. A good memoirist gets the reader to experience the artist's past life through his words, a great memoirist seduces the reader into a place where the reader suddenly recognizes the universal experiences in our shared lives. <br><br>There were parts of the book I felt like Tobias Wolff was not writing his history, but mine. The details of our lives might have been different, our stories might be adolescent antipoles, but I read Wolff and I think he has robbed me of my emotions, faked my youthful hope, slandered my stripling reputation, and squandered all of my schoolboy potential. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2642073423080083294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5550397-britany?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5550397-britany?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5550397-britany?shelf=books-to-film">books-to-film</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5550397-britany?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer762469097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2303815393523621963">This Boy's Life is a memoir about the author Tobias Wolff. Although, for most of this book he picks a different name-- Jack. I immediately got swept up in the life of Jack and his mother as they leave place after place, boyfriend after boyfriend. We start as they leave from Florida to head to Utah and we quickly understand the instability of Jack's life. Eventually she re-marries a crazy man named Dwight, who constantly is on some power trip and takes control of Jack's life. That is, until he ta</span>
  <span id="freeText2303815393523621963" style="display:none">This Boy's Life is a memoir about the author Tobias Wolff. Although, for most of this book he picks a different name-- Jack. I immediately got swept up in the life of Jack and his mother as they leave place after place, boyfriend after boyfriend. We start as they leave from Florida to head to Utah and we quickly understand the instability of Jack's life. Eventually she re-marries a crazy man named Dwight, who constantly is on some power trip and takes control of Jack's life. That is, until he takes it back.<br /><br />I enjoyed this memoir-- it hit a point where I realized we were only getting a snippet of Wolff's life. This book kept moving and we didn't move forward in Jack's life. I can understand spending this time developing Jack's rationale for some of his decisions and looking back to evaluate what impacted his life. It just felt stuck for me towards the end, then a quick summary of what happened next didn't feel fluid as the rest of the book did. I enjoyed the antics, but ultimately left unsatisfied. This book now struck a chord with all the gun violence and Nazi sentiments to this current point in our history. It was an interesting juxtaposition given the environment today and history's cyclical performance. My first book by Mr. Wolff and a peek into his juvenile life that made him successful today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2303815393523621963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_48801409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48801409" />
    <a title="Helen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1292594-helen"><img alt="Helen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230907974p2/1292594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48801409?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helen" href="/user/show/1292594-helen">Helen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48801409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8859951830245045290">This memoir would be overwhelmingly sad for me, had I not already read Old School by the same author and know that he becomes a successful author and teacher of literature at Stanford. But if you didn’t know that this child redeems himself in the end, this would be sad, a sad tale indeed. <br/><br/> Tobias’ parents divorced when he was a young boy, and his mother set off looking for a better life, leaving her oldest son with her ex-husband. In 1955 it was hard for a single mother, and life treated Tobias’</span>
  <span id="freeText8859951830245045290" style="display:none">This memoir would be overwhelmingly sad for me, had I not already read Old School by the same author and know that he becomes a successful author and teacher of literature at Stanford. But if you didn’t know that this child redeems himself in the end, this would be sad, a sad tale indeed. <br /><br /> Tobias’ parents divorced when he was a young boy, and his mother set off looking for a better life, leaving her oldest son with her ex-husband. In 1955 it was hard for a single mother, and life treated Tobias’ mother no better than the next. When the hard-scrabble life started to wear her down, she married a man who turned out to be selfish, mean, manipulative and vindictive.<br /><br />While the challenges of the everyday events in Tobias’ life make for compelling reading, his honesty about his reactions to those events are what make the book worthy of the Pen Faulkner Award. He falls in with the unruly kids and makes terrible choices. But at the same time there is a bizarre innocence about him that simultaneously disappoints and endears. For instance, after scratching an unmentionable phrase on a bathroom wall, in his fervor to convince others of his innocence, he manages to convince himself as well.<br /><br />Insight into his the lies he told and why he told them is, for me, the most fascinating aspect of This Boy’s Life. At the same time you want to strangle him for his deceits, his lies are what ultimately save him. He completely fabricates an application to a private boarding school, where he goes on full scholarship; despite the fact that he is a very poor student with nothing to recommend him. He is eventually expelled for poor grades, but while there he engages with a teacher or two who recognize that he is not lazy or stupid, but too far behind academically to keep up.<br /><br />As the novel comes to a close, Wolff is headed off to war. Yet instead of ending there, the story flashes back to a time when he was, if not happy, at least hopeful. <br /><br />Readers who enjoyed Rick Bragg’s All Over But the Shoutin’ will appreciate Wolff’s family portrait.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8859951830245045290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2305763608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2025916553443702459">This has to go straight to my favorites of all time. The prose is as generous and truthful as anything I've ever read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_442389268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/442389268" />
    <a title="Cindy Knoke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5908500-cindy-knoke"><img alt="Cindy Knoke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1398312982p2/5908500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/442389268?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cindy Knoke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cindy Knoke" href="/user/show/5908500-cindy-knoke">Cindy Knoke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer442389268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15781464463229797393">The four best memoirs I have ever read, and I have read too many, are Frank McCort’s, Angela’s Ashes, “Tobias Wolff’s, “This Boy’s Life,” Geoffrey Wolff’s, “The Duke of Deception,” and Jeanette Walls, “The Glass Castle.”<br/><br/>These books are similar in describing horrendous childhood’s of upheaval and instability, complicated by mentally ill, vagabond, eccentric parents, and a sort of lower middle class poverty. (I know that’s an oxymoron, read the books and you’ll understand). But the similarities go</span>
  <span id="freeText15781464463229797393" style="display:none">The four best memoirs I have ever read, and I have read too many, are Frank McCort’s, Angela’s Ashes, “Tobias Wolff’s, “This Boy’s Life,” Geoffrey Wolff’s, “The Duke of Deception,” and Jeanette Walls, “The Glass Castle.”<br /><br />These books are similar in describing horrendous childhood’s of upheaval and instability, complicated by mentally ill, vagabond, eccentric parents, and a sort of lower middle class poverty. (I know that’s an oxymoron, read the books and you’ll understand). But the similarities go much further and deeper. Each author is a brilliant writer with an uncanny ability to recount his or her traumatic childhoods without self-pity. They don’t seem to hold resentment towards their incompetent parents. In fact they are able to recognize the strengths in their parent’s oddity and the positive aspects of their personalities. They find in their chaotic childhood experience, grist for creative tour-de-forces, in each of these four memoirs.<br /><br />Please see prior review of “Glass Castle”. I will review “Angela’s Ashes,” soon.<br /><br />Tobias and Geoffrey Wolff are brothers. Geoffrey is eight years older than Tobias. When their parents divorce, Geoffrey heads off to live with his father and Tobias goes with his mother.<br /><br />Arthur Wolff was a Yalie, fighter pilot, and ersatz aviation engineer, who was also a vagabond, con-man, flim-flam-man, forger and alcoholic. In the “”The Duke of Deception,” Geoffrey describes his chaotic life with his crazy father who bilks and cons everyone he meets, including friends, associates, wives, and Geoffrey himself. They move from place to place in continuous flight from debtors and jail. (They end up in La Jolla, where I was born and living at the time, with my father named Arthur and brother named Jeffrey.) Arthur forges credentials and lands a job as an aeronautical engineer at General Dynamics, where my friends parents worked at the time. Eventually Arthur is committed to a mental hospital and Geoffrey heads off to Princeton.<br /><br />Geoffrey’s descriptions of his father are brilliantly nuanced, remarkably sympathetic, and psychologically insightful. He says for example, ”As I dislike him more and more. I become more and more like him. I felt trapped.” This is a remarkable statement. As a therapist one of the most difficult things to get across to people is the concept that without significant insight and effort, one tends to possess the very attributes of their own parents that they most despise. Geoffrey masters this in three short sentences.<br /><br />Tobias Wolff’s book starts in 1955 with ten year old Tobias, fleeing in a Nash Rambler that was continuously boiling over, with his mother, who was leaving one of a series of continuously violent relationships. They were driving from Florida to Utah and had broken down once again on the top of the Continental Divide, when a semi looses it’s brakes, screams it’s air horn in one long wail, and flies off the divide with Tobias and his mother watching. Tobias’s mother was moving to Florida to strike it rich mining uranium.<br /><br />So starts Tobias’s memoir. Honestly, I don’t understand the appeal of fiction as much anymore, when non-fiction is so much weirder, more incredible, and far more interesting. Tobias eventually ends up living in a town called Concrete (Washington) with a concrete, blockhead of a stepfather, who was a sadistic, martinet. Eventually he escapes all this chaos into the relatively more predictable Vietnam War and training in the special forces. (He wrote a great book about his tour of duty entitled, “In the Pharaoh’s Army: Memoirs of the Lost War.)<br /><br />Tobias and Geoffrey meet up once, after a six-year separation in La Jolla, just before Geoffrey leaves for Princeton, after their father is institutionalized. Tobias comes out by bus. Geoffrey spends the summer writing technical manuals for General Dynamic’s under his father’s name, while assigning Tobias daily reading requirements of all the Greek tragedies. I was younger at this time swimming at Windansea, right next to where they lived.<br /><br />Geoffrey eventually goes on to receive his Ph.D. in Literature, was nominated for a Pulitzer Prize, and was a Professor of Literature at University of California Irvine. He has published numerous highly acclaimed books. He had two sons and married a Clinical Social Worker. (I am a Clinical Social Worker. Weird coincidences). Tobias studied at Oxford, received his Master’s Degree in Creative Writing at Stanford and is a Professor of Creative Writing at Stanford. He also has written many highly acclaimed books. He is married and has three children. A movie was made of the book, “This Boy’s Life,” starring Leonardo What’s His Name. Their mother eventually became President of the League of Women Voters. Truth is stranger than fiction.<br /><br />The relationship between the brothers remained close and mutually supportive since their time together in La Jolla. Both are considered two of America’s finest contemporary writers.<br /><br />It is remarkable and comforting to realize that all four of these authors overcame childhood’s of shocking hardship and trauma, and used their experiences to write creative, beautiful, and inspiring memoirs.<br /><br />Highly recommend all four of these books. Recommend you read them in chronological order starting with “Angela’s Ashes,” then “The Duke of Deception,” “This Boys Life,” and “The Glass Castle.” (Toss in Pharaoh’s Army and you’ll be glad you did!)<br /><br />Happy Reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15781464463229797393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1961229131" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1961229131" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4968643-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1297550460p2/4968643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1961229131?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/4968643-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18998751-this-boy-s-life">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1961229131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer426904232448260">The opening acknowledgements had me thinking this would be a breezy comic memoir ("My first stepfather used to say that what I didn’t know would fill a book. Well, here it is.") It's very funny and moves well throughout but to say it's breezy is a gross mischaracterization.<br/><br/>The book takes its title from an adaptation of the official magazine of the Boy Scouts of America (Boy's Life), one of the seemingly few positive influences on the author when he was younger (i.e. the Scouts, not the magazine)</span>
  <span id="freeText426904232448260" style="display:none">The opening acknowledgements had me thinking this would be a breezy comic memoir ("My first stepfather used to say that what I didn’t know would fill a book. Well, here it is.") It's very funny and moves well throughout but to say it's breezy is a gross mischaracterization.<br /><br />The book takes its title from an adaptation of the official magazine of the Boy Scouts of America (Boy's Life), one of the seemingly few positive influences on the author when he was younger (i.e. the Scouts, not the magazine). The events take place somewhere between 1955 and 1960 when the author was about 10 years old and older. The book was first published in 1989, much later than the events described within. There aren't extensive quotes but enough to give me some pause, which accounts for the 1 star deduction.<br /><br />The word brutal comes to mind when I reflect on the book. Brutally funny ("He leaned forward as the bubbles rose over the Champagne Orchestra and Lawrence Welk came onstage salaaming in every direction, crying out declarations of humility in his unctuous, brain-scalding Swedish kazoo of a voice."), brutally honest ("I didn’t need to see the tears in Mr. Welch’s eyes to know that I had brought shame on myself. I knew it when we first drove into the farmyard and I saw the place in the light of day. Everything I saw thereafter forced the knowledge in deeper. These people weren’t making it. They were near the edge, and I had nudged them that much farther along. Not much, but enough to take away some of their margin. Returning the gas didn’t change that. The real harm was in their knowing that someone could come upon them in this state, and pause to do them injury. It had to make them feel small and alone, knowing this—that was the harm we had done. I understood some of this and felt the rest."). <br /><br />Wolff presents himself and his cohorts as unflinching scoundrels. Very few people get a pass including his first stepfather Dwight who is at all times comical, inept, frightening and pathetic. Several other adults come in for their own honest treatment. Wolff has a perceptive eye and an almost magical ability to describe his insights. <br /><br />I was prepared to be entertained but didn't expect the book to stir in me such a wide range of emotions. I'm glad it did.<br /><br />Note: Goodreads has added a feature where the kindle notes are available in the link below. I don't think any of my highlighted notes are spoilers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="426904232448260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_253794856" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Melody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/369112-melody"><img alt="Melody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291050708p2/369112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer8028236798947775301">Exquisitely written, desperately honest memoir that was incredibly difficult to read. I had to keep walking away, but I kept coming back for more. I am coming away from this wanting to read every word Wolff ever wrote, right away. He's so penetratingly analytical, so able to distance himself from his adolescent pretensions without disavowing the, so incisive and so true. He broke my heart, over and over and over. The prose is knife-like, crystalline and icy. I recommend it.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_345103595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345103595" />
    <a title="Alexis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10097697-alexis"><img alt="Alexis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373211941p2/10097697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345103595?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexis" href="/user/show/10097697-alexis">Alexis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345103595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7584914699679230146">In a time when conversations about creative nonfiction are preoccupied by concerns of truth in memoir, the nature of consciousness, identity, and fragmentation, it can be easy to forget about fundamentals like story. Important in and of themselves, these big conversations signal that a necessary codification of the genre has emerged and continues to grow. <br/><br/>But the meta conversations can get tedious, grandiose, even absurd, and sometimes we need to remember that readers come for the story and stay</span>
  <span id="freeText7584914699679230146" style="display:none">In a time when conversations about creative nonfiction are preoccupied by concerns of truth in memoir, the nature of consciousness, identity, and fragmentation, it can be easy to forget about fundamentals like story. Important in and of themselves, these big conversations signal that a necessary codification of the genre has emerged and continues to grow. <br /><br />But the meta conversations can get tedious, grandiose, even absurd, and sometimes we need to remember that readers come for the story and stay if it’s well done. Wolff’s memoir serves as a reminder of this simplicity—that a great book needs only to kick ass as a story, and by story I mean a million indefinable nuances that have nothing necessarily to do with high drama or theatrical events (unless of course these things are well rendered). So what else do I mean by story? In a strict sense, I mean all of the elements that a fiction writer pays careful attention to: point of view, dialogue, pacing, energy, language, characterization, mood, setting—literary analysis 101 vocabulary.<br /><br />In contrast to fiction (but not too stark a contrast), CNF can offer reflection, lyricism, musing, and the opportunity to render a mind at work and play, and I love these aspects of the genre. However, these complexities shouldn’t be explored at the expense of story. Simply basking in story is a fundamental satisfaction that reading and writing provides. <br /><br />And that’s what I did all through This Boy’s Life: I basked in its mastery. I basked in dialogue so lucid it sang; basked in energetic, fresh language; became completely absorbed by cinematic atmosphere, scene, and action; basked in spare, powerful moments of insight; pacing; voice, and on and on. The book reminded me that a great book doesn’t have to be stuffed with post-modern bells and whistles. Delivering a story in a way that absorbs the reader completely is enough. If my boiling down of all craft advice to rendering STORY seems a simplification, that’s only because Wolff makes it look so easy. <br /><br />I was in love with the book on the first page, and it kept yielding as I went—heartbreak and humor and snappy scenes and rich, sepia-toned 1950s atmosphere. Wolff’s story never lags or loses itself; from the first scene, when a tractor trailer truck loses its breaks and young Toby “Jack” Wolff and mom watch it careen off the road, the reader is hooked: "The sound got louder and then a big truck came around the corner and shot past us into the next curve, its trailer shimmying wildly. We stared after it. ‘Oh, Toby,’ my mother said, ‘he’s lost his brakes’" (3). The scene goes on and continues to be captivating, but so much is accomplished in these three short sentences. The lean description and action is just enough to engage us immediately, and we learn a lot about his mother and their relationship just from this small, but powerful, moment. We learn a lot about Mom—her tenderness, her helplessness—just from the way she says “Oh, Toby.” And of course the truck’s failure offers a wonderfully sardonic metaphor for their lives. <br /><br />One of the things I loved best about this book was its mix of alacrity and darkness. This admixture of a sweet optimism for life in spite all evidence to the contrary won me over with its authenticity. Wolff is also a great humanist—able to render the impossibility of life, its tangle of hope and disappointment. Just after the tractor truck scene, we are located in this delicious, impossible journey—both physically and emotionally: “It was 1955 and we were driving from Florida to Utah, to get away from a man my mother was afraid of and to get rich on uranium. We were going to change our luck (4). 					<br /><br />Other elements I basked in: Wolff’s use of insight—without breaking the spell or stepping outside of the evocative voice and atmosphere of his boyhood; his quirky, effective use of dialogue; and his rich, revealing characterizations of others and the self. Here’s how Wolff begins his second chapter: “I didn’t come to Utah to be the same boy I’d been before. I had my own dreams of transformation, Western dreams, dreams of freedom and dominion and taciturn self-sufficiency. The first thing I wanted to do was change my name. A girl named Toby had joined my class before I left Florida, and this had caused both of us scalding humiliation” (8). Here we have digested reflection, but still with enough boyish simplicity and suggestion, that the spell isn’t broken, and we are back to the story—changing his name to Jack, catechism, their early (and short-lived) experiences in Utah.<br /><br />Throughout the book Wolff provides such moments of insight; they come in quick, infrequent, terse strikes of language that (if we consider Hemingway’s iceberg theory) carry with them layers of suggestion and deep resonance. Upon attending his first Catholic confession, for example, young Wolff struggles with summoning a list of sins. And what’s interesting, tragic, and utterly winning about this moment is that he struggles not with the concept that he is bad, a sinner, but with the practicality of delivering a list. He takes a break from confession to confer with a young nun who mistakenly assumes he is afraid to tell on himself; she offers him a list of her own sins, and he simply steals them and offers them up as his own. It’s a wonderful, searing moment because he desperately wants to confide in her—not some arbitrary list of wrongdoing—but from a deeper well of feeling and self-loathing that he has no language for: “ I saw an anxious woman of about my mother’s age who wanted to help me without knowing what kind of help I needed. My eyes burned and my throat swelled up. I would have surrendered to her if only I’d known how” (20). This scene (and many others) made me ache with all the great humanist impulses--longing, humor, regret, tenderness, the full catastrophe.<br /><br />There are so many other examples, examples of when he implicates himself and wins over the reader: there’s the moment he forges transcripts to get into boarding school, or the moment he freezes and is unable to apologize to Mr. Welch, the farmer. But my favorite moment occurs when falls down an embankment while partying with friends and decides to stay lost for the evening, even as he can hear them calling his name. In this scene we get to see a bit not only of the scheming boy we have come to love, but also a bit of the man he might become—flawed, grandiose, and romantic: "I was happy that night, listening to them search for me, listening to them call my name. I knew they wouldn’t find me. After they went away I lay there smiling in my perfect place. Through the ferns above me I saw the nimbus of the moon in the dense, dark sky. Cool beads of water rolled down the ferns onto my face. I could just make out the sounds of the game going on up the hill, the cheers, the drumming of feet in the stands. I listened with godly condescension. I was all alone where no one could find me, only the faint excitements of a game and some voices crying Concrete, Concrete, Concrete"(191).<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7584914699679230146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75391678" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75391678" />
    <a title="Monty J Heying" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2867071-monty-j-heying"><img alt="Monty J Heying" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494455380p2/2867071.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75391678?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monty J Heying" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monty J Heying" href="/user/show/2867071-monty-j-heying">Monty J Heying</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2867071-monty-j-heying?shelf=biography">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75391678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17480052335024714292">To write a memoir is to sift through and make sense one's personal experience that were laid down in our heads when we were much younger, then arrange them into a compelling and comprehensible story. Reframing is necessary because the writer has matured. The eyes behind the pen are not the eyes that witnessed what is being written. This book is a mature and evolved retrospective about coming of age in the American 1950-60s.<br/><br/>It's a good book that I appreciated more fully after I saw the movie (Rob</span>
  <span id="freeText17480052335024714292" style="display:none">To write a memoir is to sift through and make sense one's personal experience that were laid down in our heads when we were much younger, then arrange them into a compelling and comprehensible story. Reframing is necessary because the writer has matured. The eyes behind the pen are not the eyes that witnessed what is being written. This book is a mature and evolved retrospective about coming of age in the American 1950-60s.<br /><br />It's a good book that I appreciated more fully after I saw the movie (Robert DiNiro and Leonardo DiCaprio.) The writing's straightforward, honest and clean. There's no trickery, no self-pity or bragging, nothing flowery or poetic, but there is a wealth of cultural commentary on the people and times. The book is courageously candid in many places.<br /><br />The curse and the challenge of the memoir form is that an author is weighed down with an abundance of history that must be condensed, massaged or cut away in order to present a comprehensible story. Given the sheer scope of the decade or so covered, Wolff's done a pretty amazing job of harvesting into a mere 300 pages.<br /><br />There's great dialogue throughout, especially the confession and the arcade scene with a barker named "Smoke." <br /><br />I was surprised that halfway through I began rooting for the narrator, a kid that I'd found spoiled and unlikable in the beginning.<br /><br />Other than the narrator/author/protagonist, the characters who stand out are Toby's devoted mother (we should all be so blessed), a school friend named Arthur, and Toby's villainous stepfather Dwight. Arthur provides psychological insights beyond his years and supplies the key to Toby and his mother's escape from the clutches of Dwight. Dwight provides high drama, a tortured soul so consumed with his own unmet developmental needs for attention that he cannot function as a loving partner or stepfather.<br /><br />Both Frank Conroy and Wolff weave in after-the-fact commentary with the contemporaneous story-telling, a technique which, though necessary and instructive, I found temporarily pulled me out of the story in a few places. But not for long.<br /><br />This is a must read for anyone interested in the American 1950-60s, the post-Korean War/pre-Vietnam War era of "innocence."</span>
  <a data-text-id="17480052335024714292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1261107329?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emer Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emer Martin" href="/user/show/1713135-emer-martin">Emer Martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713135-emer-martin?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1261107329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4961777546878620938">I was struck reading other reviews of this book that many stated that it read so well it could have been a novel and not a memoir. What struck me also was that he chose some incidents that showed him in an awful light, beating a poor frightened dog with a mop, being one of them. However, Wolff is such a good writer I didn't care how many dogs he beat. The voice in the book carried me through the relative mundane, rainy, overcast world of a grim childhood in the gloom of Washington State. The loc</span>
  <span id="freeText4961777546878620938" style="display:none">I was struck reading other reviews of this book that many stated that it read so well it could have been a novel and not a memoir. What struck me also was that he chose some incidents that showed him in an awful light, beating a poor frightened dog with a mop, being one of them. However, Wolff is such a good writer I didn't care how many dogs he beat. The voice in the book carried me through the relative mundane, rainy, overcast world of a grim childhood in the gloom of Washington State. The local high school Concrete High is 40 miles away and in a town called Concrete because of the factory. <br />His mother makes one poor decision with men after another, but then she is a victim of her times. She is cheerful and bright and pragmatic but utterly trapped in her life as a low paid single parent waitress or office worker. This was the 1950s. One scene where all the women in the house have to go out and find men to marry and protect them is especially sad. You sense they would all have been better off living together in that house and raising their kids without the men they brought into their lives.<br />What I expected from hearing about this book was a kind of torturous account of living with an evil stepfather. This wasn't it. Even the stepfather, Dwight, feels trapped and suffocated by the dark mountains and forests.He is not painted as a monster, though he holds a knife to his mother's throat and beats up on Tobias as a kid. <br />There are parts of this book that are hilarious, Tobias grubbing for badges at Boy Scouts, his constant lying and self invention, his self professed snobbery and determination to fake enough recommendations and grades to get a scholarship and get out of there.<br />I almost jumped into the book to kick the character to Paris, when his uncle offers him a way out and a life in that city. <br />Even though this book is pure Americana, the cars, guns, boy scouts, drinking high school days etc nothing is cliched. <br />Thoughtful and poised it is a story worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4961777546878620938" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_400717168" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400717168" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=father-brother-sons-book">father-brother-sons-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=books-loved-2014">books-loved-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400717168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1076774198904034658">This is one of the greatest growing up memoirs of all time, I think, and is still taught in schools as such. It's a story by an accomplished and much celebrated writer--who came to direct the Stanford Creative Writing Program--about his completely wild and sad youth, filled with bad choices by his mom and him, with a parade of many awful men along the way. Wolff is an elegant and insightful writer, often darkly hilarious as he repeatedly depicts an assurance that he would be just fine, and very </span>
  <span id="freeText1076774198904034658" style="display:none">This is one of the greatest growing up memoirs of all time, I think, and is still taught in schools as such. It's a story by an accomplished and much celebrated writer--who came to direct the Stanford Creative Writing Program--about his completely wild and sad youth, filled with bad choices by his mom and him, with a parade of many awful men along the way. Wolff is an elegant and insightful writer, often darkly hilarious as he repeatedly depicts an assurance that he would be just fine, and very successful, facing the future with aplomb, and so on, and then having everything crash and burn again and again.<br /><br />This is heartbreakingly beautiful writing about a terrible growing up period he obviously happened to survive to write about. You smile throughout and shake your head at his insight now, and his failure of insight along the way, and his complete lack of models for growing up. It's not clear how he made it, actually. He makes it clear he was without luck, without character, without a compass. But you can't look away, you can't stop reading, it's that exhilarating. It took me 30 years to read this (why? I have a lot of books laying around!) but it was worth the wait.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1076774198904034658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66746572" />
    <a title="J.I." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/310055-j-i"><img alt="J.I." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1303018958p2/310055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/66746572?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.I." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.I." href="/user/show/310055-j-i">J.I.</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/310055-j-i?shelf=read-2009">read-2009</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66746572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9178141439332305404">Tobias Wolff is a man known to those who love short stories as something of a master of the form. His novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11464.Old_School' title='Old School by Tobias Wolff' rel='nofollow noopener'>Old School</a> failed to capitalize on his brilliance of the short form, so I must admit to some qualms about his memoir. I couldn't have been more wrong. Wolff analyses his upbringing with the clarity of an outsider, giving us insight into his deeds and (more frequently) misdeeds. From constant travels with his single mother escaping a bad relationship to an abusive step father, from mocking</span>
  <span id="freeText9178141439332305404" style="display:none">Tobias Wolff is a man known to those who love short stories as something of a master of the form. His novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11464.Old_School" title="Old School by Tobias Wolff" rel="nofollow noopener">Old School</a> failed to capitalize on his brilliance of the short form, so I must admit to some qualms about his memoir. I couldn't have been more wrong. Wolff analyses his upbringing with the clarity of an outsider, giving us insight into his deeds and (more frequently) misdeeds. From constant travels with his single mother escaping a bad relationship to an abusive step father, from mocking his best friend mercilously to cheating his way into boarding school, Wolff doesn't try to make himself look good or even to justify his actions. He let's them speak for themselves while providing insight into what he was thinking. Great stuff</span>
  <a data-text-id="9178141439332305404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

            Shelves:

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8707630-marie-gentilcore?shelf=sync">sync</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1648159625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10579207376262202885">3.5 stars. It was an interesting and well written memoir. It's funny how listening to another's life experiences make you think about your own experiences and those of others. My dad is a few years older than Tobias and some of the things Tobias wrote about mirrors stories my dad has told about times spent with his friends when he was a boy and teen. Now I'm I'll have to check out the movie adaptation that stars a very young Leonardo DiCaprio and Robert DeNiro.</span>
