<div id="review_3543870131" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3543870131" />
    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/89283877-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595000452p2/89283877.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3543870131?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/89283877-claire">Claire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=not-a-fan">not-a-fan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=abuse">abuse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=new-author">new-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=has-movie-adaption">has-movie-adaption</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=missing-something">missing-something</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=set-decades-ago">set-decades-ago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89283877-claire?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3543870131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12123103832358249596">"This is a book of memory, and memory has its own story to tell. But I have done my best to make it tell a truthful story.<br/>My first step father used to say that what I didn't know would fill a book. Well, here it is."<br/><br/>This book was just gloomy.<br/><br/>I'm disappointed to be honest. I will admit that the only reason I read this book/had ever heard of it, was because I wanted to read it before watching the 1993 movie starring Robert DeNiro and Leonardo DiCaprio (two of my favourite actors), so I hadn't spec</span>
  <span id="freeText12123103832358249596" style="display:none">"This is a book of memory, and memory has its own story to tell. But I have done my best to make it tell a truthful story.<br />My first step father used to say that what I didn't know would fill a book. Well, here it is."<br /><br />This book was just gloomy.<br /><br />I'm disappointed to be honest. I will admit that the only reason I read this book/had ever heard of it, was because I wanted to read it before watching the 1993 movie starring Robert DeNiro and Leonardo DiCaprio (two of my favourite actors), so I hadn't specifically chosen this book because of the author/storyline.<br /><br />This memoir opens in 1955 with Tobias Wolff at just ten years old following his parents' divorce. In order to leave his past identity behind, Tobias changes his name to Jack as he and his mother head to Utah in search for a better life. But it isn't long before Jack becomes a target of his new stepfather Dwight's relentless hostility. This book follows Jack's journey through adolescence as he attempts multiple outrageous schemes to find his place in the world, and get as far from Dwight as he can.<br /><br />This book should've been good, but Jack just wasn't likeable. I'll have to say fair play to the author for this, to be able to write his own memoir showing himself in bad light, holding nothing back. Maybe writing this book was the redemption I was looking for. Let's just go with that. <br /><br />Obviously, I feel sorry for Jack. No one ever deserves to live in the type of circumstances he was in: with an alcoholic, verbally and occasionally physically abusive stepfather. I can clearly see how this would affect Jack's character, so he's definitely not fully to blame for his actions. But I just can't ignore his actions and fully blame them on his circumstances. Jack is a pathalogical liar, thief and a conman, and these factors definitely took from my empathy for his story. <br /><br />****************SPOILERS***************<br /><br />This book ends after Jack has been expelled from the prep school he spent a good portion of this book trying to get into. He's failed his classes, and become dependent on alcohol and cigarettes to complete the perfect stereotype of a school dropout. Why on earth did the book end here? Throughout the book, there was foreshadowing that Jack had grown up and gotten married, had children himself. And yet the book simply ends with him being a complete failure? <br /><br />*************SPOILERS OVER************<br /><br />Usually, memoirs are written after the author has had their redemption, and wants to share their experiences with others to be inspirational, moving, even as a warning to not fall into the same traps. I understand that this was a story Wolff wanted to tell for his own sake, but I have no idea why he wouldn't include his redemption in the book. For me, this made the book end on a sour, negative note.<br /><br />This book was written well, and I did find the story and descriptions of post-war America interesting. I'm giving this book 3🌟 because I think I would've been much happier with this book if it wasn't for the anticlimactic ending, and I try to not judge books solely on their endings. I'm really excited to watch the movie now, I can't wait to see DeNiro and DiCaprio bring this story to life. I'm expecting to enjoy the movie more, especially since I really like the actors.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12123103832358249596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3376354013" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3376354013" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10770371-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540580928p2/10770371.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3376354013?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/10770371-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10770371-dan?shelf=best-books-of-the-west">best-books-of-the-west</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3376354013" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17868872631974440036">This Boy’s Life is a boyhood memoir set primarily in Washington state in the 1950’s. It was penned in 1987. <br/><br/><b>We hated each other. We hated each other so much that other feelings didn’t get enough light. It disfigured me. When I think of Chinook [Washington] I have to search for the faces of my friends, their voices, the rooms where I was made welcome. But I can always see Dwight’s [stepdad] face and hear his voice. I hear his voice in my own when I speak to my children in anger. They hear it too,</b></span>
  <span id="freeText17868872631974440036" style="display:none">This Boy’s Life is a boyhood memoir set primarily in Washington state in the 1950’s. It was penned in 1987. <br /><br /><b>We hated each other. We hated each other so much that other feelings didn’t get enough light. It disfigured me. When I think of Chinook [Washington] I have to search for the faces of my friends, their voices, the rooms where I was made welcome. But I can always see Dwight’s [stepdad] face and hear his voice. I hear his voice in my own when I speak to my children in anger. They hear it too, and look at me in surprise. My youngest once said, “Don’t you love me anymore?”</b><br /><br />This Boy’s Life was Hillbilly Elegy thirty years before Hillbilly Elegy. There are obvious parallels between the two memoirs. Abusive stepdads, moms that are trapped in cycles of poverty and who marry men half of their intellect, and of course boys who overcome significant hurdles to escape their small towns and ominous fates. Also both memoirs spawned Hollywood films - Hillbilly Elegy is due out soon. <br /><br />This Boy’s Life is the better written of the two memoirs. It is much more than a catharsis chronicling a teenage boy’s growing pains and mischief making. It reads like a candid slice of America — not your 1950’s Leave it to Beaver drivel. And there is more than enough self-effacing and wry humor to make up for the dark moments. Some scenes are laugh out loud funny. <br /><br />The book ends somewhat abruptly when Wolff is sixteen and is sent out East to a prep school that he lies his way into. He commits a few lines to the fact that he was booted out around the time his father was committed to a mental instituition. I wondered about this and thought that he was focused solely on his childhood in Washington maybe for a tidier wrap-up but I read recently that Wolff’s brother Geoffrey, also a well known writer, had already written about his own prep school experience and their father’s mental illness. <br /><br />5 stars. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17868872631974440036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_259081285" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/259081285" />
    <a title="Jan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/386247-jan"><img alt="Jan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/259081285?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan" href="/user/show/386247-jan">Jan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer259081285" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16349210795633022483">Wolff’s memoir of his nomadic, fatherless childhood searching for an identity and a future is hypnotically engaging. In search of wealth and the right man, his divorced mother moved Toby, who renamed himself Jack, from Florida to Utah to Washington State, where she married Dwight, definitely the wrong man, especially for Jack. "I was bound to accept as my home a place I did not feel at home in,” he writes, “and to take as my father a man who was offended by my existence and would never stop ques</span>
  <span id="freeText16349210795633022483" style="display:none">Wolff’s memoir of his nomadic, fatherless childhood searching for an identity and a future is hypnotically engaging. In search of wealth and the right man, his divorced mother moved Toby, who renamed himself Jack, from Florida to Utah to Washington State, where she married Dwight, definitely the wrong man, especially for Jack. "I was bound to accept as my home a place I did not feel at home in,” he writes, “and to take as my father a man who was offended by my existence and would never stop questioning my right to it.” <br /><br />Jack descends with no remorse into a delinquency that includes drinking, lying, stealing, destruction of property, and ultimately, submitting falsified documents to get into a private school. Without sparing the details of his misdeeds, the adult Wolff keeps us on the boy Jack’s side. I was halfway through the book before I realized that the pathetic bully Dwight wasn’t the only Bad Guy—just one old enough to know better, unlike his stepson. I thought of all the rebellious, unreachable boys I’d butted heads with as a teacher and understood Dwight’s frustration with this unlovable kid who was the price of getting the woman he wanted. Even a kinder, gentler stepfather might have failed to tame such a boy. <br /><br />But Wolff makes us understand that at the heart of a bad kid is the legitimate, if misguided, search for some kind of personal power. Watching World War II movies, Jack knew that “the point of the show was to celebrate the victory of goodness over evil….[But] the real point was to celebrate snappy uniforms and racy Mercedes staff cars and great marching, thousands of boots slamming down together,… to watch Stukas peel off and dive toward burning cities, tanks blowing holes in buildings, men with Lugers and dogs ordering people around. These shows instructed us further in the faith we were already beginning to hold: that victims are contemptible, no matter how much people pretend otherwise; that it is more fun to be inside than outside, to be arrogant than to be kind, to be with a crowd than to be alone.” That’s the trouble with being a victim, Wolff reminds us—it can make you aspire to tyranny.<br /><br />Jack ultimately escapes to the private school he lied his way into, but can’t succeed there either and is eventually thrown out. He “wore himself out with raging” and then joined the army. Wolff ends the chronology there, Jack still unredeemed. But in the final passage he relives a nighttime ride with his friend Chuck. Believing they have broken free of their wrong-filled pasts and are on the lip of a limitless future, they break into song. “It was a good night to sing and we sang for all we were worth, as if we’d been saved.”<br /><br />Jack/Toby grew up to be one of America’s most respected writers and teachers. I wish he would write a sequel; I want to know the details of the continued transformation from a powerless boy to a man who, having found himself, empowers others. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16349210795633022483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2044602496" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2044602496" />
    <a title="Colton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26491281-colton"><img alt="Colton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493840095p2/26491281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2044602496?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colton" href="/user/show/26491281-colton">Colton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26491281-colton?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2044602496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1108409703646872470">Wolff is quickly becoming one of my favorite writers, having read a novel, a novella, a short story collection, and now a memoir. And man, he can write in any form, and better than most anyone else. <br/><br/>Reading this after reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/753345.Old_School' title='Old School by Tobias Wolff' rel='nofollow noopener'>Old School</a>, Wolff's novel about a prep school boy competing with his classmates to win private audiences with their literary heroes, you can see where some of the inspiration was drawn. The narrator in Old School is undoubtedly modeled somewhat after Wolff's own experiences</span>
  <span id="freeText1108409703646872470" style="display:none">Wolff is quickly becoming one of my favorite writers, having read a novel, a novella, a short story collection, and now a memoir. And man, he can write in any form, and better than most anyone else. <br /><br />Reading this after reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/753345.Old_School" title="Old School by Tobias Wolff" rel="nofollow noopener">Old School</a>, Wolff's novel about a prep school boy competing with his classmates to win private audiences with their literary heroes, you can see where some of the inspiration was drawn. The narrator in Old School is undoubtedly modeled somewhat after Wolff's own experiences at the Hill School, which he doesn't go into in This Boy's Life, though he does detail how he got in. <br /><br />This Boy's Life is as good as a memoir gets. That is, it reads like a novel, and Wolff treats himself like a character. There's not a whole lot of reminiscing, or wallowing, or preachy moments where Wolff tries to make us feel bad for him. More or less, you feel like you're reading a novel. And a damn good one. <br /><br />The book follows young Tobias Wolff, who changes his name to Jack, as he navigates adolescence. That sounds like a cheesy teen movie description, but it's anything but. Jack isn't just worried about making friends and getting girls, he's evading his mom's abusive husband, ranking up in the Boy Scouts, delivering papers, husking chestnuts, manipulating his way into the upper class, shoplifting, drinking, and fighting. And he's trying to make friends and get girls too. <br /><br />I laughed out loud several times, like when Jack and his friends Terry and Terry are throwing eggs at an unsuspecting man in a new Thunderbird. I nearly cried a couple of times too, like when Jack's stepdad Dwight hits him after he didn't clean out the mustard bottle to his satisfaction. The range of emotion Wolff packs in to the memoir is astounding. It's never one-note, and you never feel just one way about anything that happens. Simply put, Wolff is writing about a life. Not a moment or a situation, but a life as a whole. <br /><br />How much of a memoir is factually true, who's to say? But it doesn't matter. I know I loved it, and loved thinking that perhaps Dwight was out there somewhere, reading This Boy's Life, and knowing the world now knows what a monster he is. But, as Wolff wrote him, he probably thinks he was the catalyst for the great writer that Jack became. And maybe he was. Maybe that's the point. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1108409703646872470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('T4jyTj9bjcu29oWSdcJyp9yTfm7AddCsuj1HhfZ+tq8j9mKijnGSsxxnXSfqVnz4x+M4Rq4kixSYfYlWWT3Taw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer7767257196685678384">Tobias Jonathan Ansell Wolff is a writer of fiction and nonfiction.<br/><br/>He is best known for his short stories and his memoirs, although he has written two novels.<br/><br/>Wolff is the Ward W. and Priscilla B. Woods Professor in the School of Humanities and Sciences at Stanford University, where he has taught classes in English and creative writing since 1997. He also served as the director of the Creative Writ</span>
  <span id="freeText7767257196685678384" style="display:none">Tobias Jonathan Ansell Wolff is a writer of fiction and nonfiction.<br /><br />He is best known for his short stories and his memoirs, although he has written two novels.<br /><br />Wolff is the Ward W. and Priscilla B. Woods Professor in the School of Humanities and Sciences at Stanford University, where he has taught classes in English and creative writing since 1997. He also served as the director of the Creative Writing Program at Stanford from 2000 to 2002.<br /><br /></span>
